half a century ago. He was educated In
the Oakland public schools and here grew

ABE STILL REPUBLICANS.
Senator Elkins, chief of the "boxer"
contingent, said:
There is a strong sentiment in both
branches of Congress in favor of the an
nexation of the island. Senator Platt of
New' York said to-day that he was posi
tive that a hill to annex Cuba would pass
both houses. He admitted that the initia
tive would have to come from the Cubans
and believes that sentiment in favor of
annexation would spread rapidly in Cuba.
Senator Burrows, one of the "boxers,"
was in a cheerful .mood to-day.
have nothing to gloat over," .said
he. "We have not been making war on
the administration as charged, but we
have been doing what we believe our
duty to the Republican party. We
honestly believe that the legislation pro
posed for /concessions to Cuba will be
hurtful to American interests ana" of lit
tle or no practical benefit to Cuban sugar
.growers."
ANNEXATION SENTIMENT.
Whether concessions to Cuba can be ac
complished through a commercial treaty
is uncertain. Representatives of the beet
sugar industry said to-day that a reci
procity treaty would be as objectionable
as the reciprocity bill, if it provided for
20 per cent reduction in the tariff. They
oppose a change in the existing tariff,
but would favor a commercial treaty that
would provide for a 20 per cent drawback,
rebate or bounty, to be paid by the
United States to Cuba and distributed
by. the Cuban'Government to cane grow
ers and sugar producers of the- island.
President Roosevelt, President Palma,
Secretary Root and General Wopd agree
that a rebate is impracticable and re
fuse to consider the proposition.
The result is very plainly a distinct re
buft for the administration. No explana
tion that the Cuban bill was merely a
measure' supported by Roosevelt because
President McKlnley would have advo
cated it can change the fact. .
Should this resolution be adopted, that
will end the matter. There will be no
party split and no public discussion on
the floor of the Senate.

CALL BUREAU. 1406 G STREET, N.
W., WASHINGTON, June 1".âArrange
ments for the â¢ obsequies of the Cuban
reciprocity bill were virtually perfected
to-day. In accordance with the pro
gramme outlined this morning in The
Call, which told of the death of the bill,
the Republican members of the Commit
tee on Cuba met to-da^ and adopted the
Spooner substitute. This provides for a
20 per cent reduction in tho duty on im
ports from Cuba into' the United States,
in return for an equivalent reduction by
"Cuba oft goods imported from the United
States. The reduced duty is to remain in
force not longer than five years, and the
President is required to assure -himself
that the reduction, so far as sugar and
tobacco are concerned, is to benefit thn
producers in Cuba and not purchasers in
the United States.
Special Dispatch 1 to Tbe Call.
During the afternoon the Kin? drove
out from Windsor Castle in a closed car
riage. He appeared to be In good health.
This morning it was officially an
nounced that King Edward was much bet
ter. He passed a good night and his pro
gress toward complete recovery was un
interrupted. The measures
ordered by his physicians are said to bÂ»
due to the necessity of husbanding hla
strength in view of the fatigues of the
coronation week. The King was keenlx
disappointed at being unable to attend the
Ascot Heath race meeting to-day, which
he intended to open with all the state
ceremonial of the early days of Queen
Victoria. He hopes, however, to be pres
ent Thursday. Gold Cup day.
EEFOBTED MUCH BETTEE.
At a meeting of the Irish members of
the Hou3e of Commons this afternoon
resolutions to the effect that the Irish
Nationalist?, as a -protest against the
"misgovernment of their country," re
solved to take no part in the coronation
ceremonies, and that the Irish party be
summoned to meet in Dublin on the day
of King Edward's coronation to take into
consideration the condition of Ireland,
were unanimously adopted.
At Scotland Yard to-night the utmost
reticence was maintained concerning these
rumors. " It was noticeable, however, that
the chief inspectors, who usually return
home at night, were all on duty there, and
while they refuse to see newspaper repre
sentatives until to-morrow morning, they
decline to either deny or confirm the ru
mor.
LONDON, June 17.â A sensational story
is current in London to-night of the dis
covery of a plot to assassinate Kins Ed
ward. This story has created much dis
cussion in newspaper and other circles,
but it is lacking in anything like official
confirmation.
According to the current report. King
Edward's sudden illness at Aldershot was
not due to cold, but was merelv an ex
cuse â¢ for withdrawing his Majesty from
public functions, owing to the discovery
by Scotland Yard of a plot against hl3 life.
The principals in thi3 plot have not yet
been arjrested. It is cited in confirmation
of this story that King Edward's recov
ery when he was ensconced at Windsor
Castle was as complete and speedy as hla
attack had been sudden.
On the other hand, it must be pointed out
that if his Majesty's illness was merely
diplomatic, the officials carefully took a
great deal of trouble in keepinc up the
fiction. Sir Francis Laking, physician in
ordinary to the King, was summoned by
telegraph to Aldershot; his prescriptions
were hurriedly filled, and everything about
the King's apartments indicated the gen
uine nature of the King's illness. Fur
thermore^ -King Edward's journey from
Aldershot to Windsor in his motor car and
his subsequent drive to-day in Windsor
Park do not seem to. Indicate any fear of
a further attack upon his person.

In: spite of the vigilance of the deputies
and reservation police, many "sooners"
succeeded in locating claims and lined up!
with the others. Land attorneys were
busy this afternoon investigating contests
on these claims. At 2:40 p. m. the special
train from McCammon, run for the benefit
of those who had located on claims, ar
rived in Blackfoot over the Oregon Short
Line. It was packed to suffocation. Men
were on top of the 'coaches, on the tender;
on the locomotive pilot, everywhere that
afforded a foothold. Before the train
stopped they swattried from the coaches
like bees and a mad rush for the land
office took place. For more than an hour
the force of deputies was helpless, but
finally ; succeeded in forming the crowd
into line. \ -vJ ' : Â¦Â¦-. Â» â¢;-â¢- <
Shortly after 1 o'clock the - spectators
saw a cloud of dust rapidly approaching
on the road stretching out to tne south
west. Soon two dusty, choking-, riders
were made out, whipping and spurring,
running neck and neck, on horses that
seemed ready to dsop dead from exhaus
tion. . Exactly one hour and fifteen min
utes from the time they had.left Pocatello,
twenty-six miles away, the two riders,
Mullen and Dekay, leaped Â¦ from their
horses in front of the land office. Mullen
secured the coveted first position. \ Each

Germany, in the care of medical special
ists, it became evident that recovery was
not probable and Hobbs decided to return
home to pass his last days in San Fran
cisco, where he had resided many years.

cade, numbers many of those who made
themselves rich in Thunder Mountain last
fall and who are returning to increase
their store.
News of rich strikes continues to come
in. Perhaps the most notable is that
niade by Captain W. C Bryan, who rep
resents ex-Senator Thurston of Nebraska.
None of the ore' runs "less than $200 a ton
ana much of it Is flaked with gold visible
ta the naked eye and assays as -high as
$17,000 a ton.
Notwithstanding four towns havo
sprung up as if by magic, with their small
stores being supplied every day from pack
trains, the cost of provisions remains very
high. Flour sells for $10 a sack, bacon ol>
cents a pound, dried fruit 25 cents a.'
pound, canned goods 40 cents each., -/ .

render of 915 Boers. This completes the
surrenders In the Transvaal. Under .date
of June 17 Lord Kitchener announces that
700 Boers surrendered, at Bloemfontein

men. surrendered and 10.813 rifles were

ony 5395 men surrendered and 52S0 rifles
were turned in. The figures for Cape
Colony have not been ! fully received.

Before the sun had sunk to rest on that
eventful 17th day of June, 1775, those thir
teen colonies had .found the Inspiration of a
new nation, and conceived the hope and had
established the nucleus of a glorious na
tional existence.
From the day of Bunker Hill we have never
swerved from national right and national right
dealing. Whatever territory we have acquired;
whatever history we have made; whatever ideas
and principles we have established, have been
along these lines. And God â¢ grant that none
other shall ever actuate our statesmen, our
Government or our people.
Fellow Americans, the sword of Bunker Hill
flashed a message to the nations of the earth,
a mesEage of liberty, and of national manhood;
that message has not been dimmed by. the ad
vancing years, nor has it lost any of its bright
ness or luster from its reflection; repeated
from ocean 'to ocean and from State to State.
It unites the descendants of Pilgrim Fathers
on Massachusetts rock bound shores with the
descendants of California's pioneers and with
the pioneers themselves, in this land of sun-'
shine, fruit and flowers, and on this anniver
sary day it proves the Inspiration and the oc
casion of thousands of these ' patriotic Ameri
can reunions held In every State. ':
Gentlemen of the Associated Societies, I
thank you indeed for the honor and privilege
of being with you to-day. I can be no better
American, but I shall certainly take with me
hence the most pleasant memories and the true
significance of a great and united American
ism. (Applause.)
WINNERS OF RACES.
After another patriotic selection by the
band, Miss Juliet Greninger sang' "The
Star Spangled Banner." This was fol
lowed by a cornet solo by H. Dibbin and
a tenor solo by Daniel Lawrence. Chair
man Badger read a telegram from a Bos
ton society and then called upon the audi
ence to render "America" and every voice
Joined in singing the ode. The exercises
closed with a benediction by Father
Lacey. .
After luncheon there was dancing' until
5 o'clock. The races proved very inter
esting. The winners in the various events
were: -

Weener second.
Upon arrival at the picnic grove the
literary exercises were inaugurated. The
programme opened with a grand medley
of patriotic airs by the Second Regiment
Band. This was followed by a prayer de
livered by Rev. T. J. Lacey. William G.
Badger, president of the day, delivered
some appropriate opening remarks. He
dwelt at length on the occasion that
brought them together and expressed the
hope that all would enjoy themselves. At
$Â£?â concrusion of the address Alfred
jy.UH 1 .Â® render ed "The Sword of Bunker

The president of the day then intro
duced lion. Eugene E. Schmitz as the
tor * .The Mayor was heartily cheered,
wnen the noise had ceased the city's chief
executive spoke in part as follows:
In this lovely place, where every attraction
of nature calls upon those who love her to
enjoy her gracious smiles and her delightful
welcome and to forget, for a time our city
habits and all urban restrictions a lengthy
address would. I fear, seem incongruous; yet
the spirit of the day. whose anniversary we
now celebrate and which is still so strong and
so fresh in the American heart that wherever
Americans congregate, whether in the crowded
city or in the open country; whether amid the
busy cares of life or in our moments of freest
case there is ever room for pauseâ time to
consider and to recall with wonder and with
admiration the birth of our American inde
pendence, and the growth and progress of our
American principles and our American institu
tions.
One hundred and twenty-seven years ago to
day that sun which now In splendor, shines
upon forty-six sovereign States of a united and
forever indissoluble country, rose as it were
from the eastern sea upon thirteen British col
onies, separate In their ideas, separate in their
people, and separate' in their governmental
conditions, except as they all owed allegiance
to an unloved, unjust and despotic ruler beyond
the sea. . ,

heat, but there was no way out of the
building except through the store, which
was a mass of flames.

BLOODHOUNDS ON THAIL.
They had gone but a short distance
â¢when three rifle shots eame from the
brush near the roadside, and the guards
drove for their lives. Five bullets were
gent after them. The first struck a horse
end passed under Blesecker's right arm,
cutting a clean hole through the side of
his coat; another wounded a horse in the
head, a third grazed a horse's side, and a
fourth cut through the harness. The men
reached town soon after 2 o'clock and re
ported to Sheriff Marsh.
A battle occurred about 2 o'clock this
morning between Bert Biesecker and Lon
Davis, two members of Sheriff Marsh's
posse, and Tracy and Merrill, in which
eleven rifle shots were exchanged. Bie
zsecker had a narrow escape, his clothing
'being pierced by a bullet. At 11 o'clock
last night Biesecker and Davis Baw two
men go down to Salmon Creek and get a
drink at a point about six miles north of
Vancouver. The watchers were not cer
tain of the identity of the pair and halted
until the latter again approached the
road. The guards then advanced and
when about thirty yards distant Biesecker
sent a rifle shot in the direction of the
supposed convicts. The fire was prompt
ly returned and six shots were exchanged,
rone of which took effect, so far as
known.
Both parties then took to cover. After
â¢waiting in their hiding place for more
than an hour, the guards moved up a
quarter of a mile, where their horses were
tied. They again lay in wait another half
hour, in t'he hope of seeing the fugitives,
but nothing occurred ana they got into
their burgJ' and started toward town.
The posse came up scarcely an hour
afterward and at once started in hot pur
6ult Several miles beyond Pioneer Church
the track of the fugitives was lost, but
Is Delieved they passed the main road
leading off the Pioneer road to La Center
and continued north along a trail through
the woods, which follows the Lewis River
toward Lewisville.
EABLY MORNING BATTLE.
VANCOUVER, Yfash.. June 17.â Tracy
and Merrill, the murderous convicts, who
have been chased for eight days, are still
at large to-night, but a posse in charge of
Sheriff Marsh of Clark County Is close
en their heels near La Center, a village
twenty miles from Vancouver. The fugi
tives were seen several times during the
Â«iay and at 7:30 o'clock a telephone mes
sage from Sheriff Marsh at La Center an
nounced that they had passed Pioneer
Church, six miles southeast of La Center
and fourteen miles from here, late in the
afternoon. They were on horseback, hav
ing stolen two horses from 'the farm of
John Rathburn after ' holding up Rath
burn's father-in-law, William Kauzler.
After securing the horses the outlaws
rode rapidly to Pioneer Church, two
miles west, and then turned north in the
direction of La Center and Lewisville, on
Lewis River.
The blaze started in the varnishing or
finishing room on the fourth floor and
spread with unusual quickness to the
other floors, which were packed with the
most inflammable sort of material in the
shape of furniture, rattan, baby carriages
and go-carts. For two hours the firemen
fought the flames from places of danger,
finally subduing them, but not until the
building had been rendered a mere shell
of four walls.

married woman with two young children,
but some time ago her husband deserted
her. He was regarded as a shiftless fel
low. Several days ago Moore was re
ported to have been seen In this county. It
is the opinion of a few that there la some
connection between hia reappearance and
Sunday night's affair. Mrs. Moore could
not identify her assailant in. the darkness
and was too excited to give a description
of him.
Several theories are given as to the
cause o* the assault. For the past few
weeks Nevada women have been ter
rorized by an unknown man, who at
tacks them on dark streets. It is believed
this man was the assailant of Mrs. Moore.
NEVADA, June 17.â Mrs. EUhu Moore, a
young woman of this city, was brutally
attacked by an unknown man late Sun
day night. Mrs. Moore was on her way
home from town and was passing a de
serted spot on the road when suddenly a
man appeared ahead of her. Before she
could call for help or attempt to escape
the fellow had seized her and waa at
tempting: to hurl her to the ground. Mrs.
Moore Is possessed of strength and fear
steeled her muscles. The struggle was
desperate. Finally, with an almost super
human effort, she broke from his grasp,
but not until her clothing was torn from
her body. Mrs. Moore ran up the road and
managed to elude her brutal pursuer until
she reached home. She was In an ex
hausted condition, but managed to tell her
story sufficiently to make clear the cause
of her pitiable plight.
As soon as possible the young woman's
father, Levi Nicholson, who is employed
in the Champion mine, was Informed and
a posse started in search of the assailant.
Its efforts were not successful and tha
culprit is still at large.

v ; . 7 18 Market Street- . iZZf^The Pacific Coast Horticultural Society
met last night and decided to hold the an
nual summer flower show In the grand
nave of the Ferry building from August
14 to 16. After the closing night of the
show there will be a grand promenade
concert in the ferry building. Music will
be furnished by a superb band of sixteen
pieces. The committee in charge is striv
ing to make the coming show a success,
and judging from the amount of interest
taken in the venture it should eclipse any
thing of its kind ever before held under
the auspices of the society. The society
has accepted the invitation extended by
John Rock, an extensive florist of Niles,
to visit his place on July 6. A grand re
union and outing will take place and new
achievements in the culture of flowers
will be discussed.
Plans for Annual Display in
Ferry Building.

IXORAX EXHIBITION
Al Neill and Young Gibbs have not
signed articles to fight before the Twen
tieth Century Club this month. The club
officials have not posted their forfeit and
the boxers are afraid the fight cannot be
held under the law. There will be an
other conference to-day. -
Ed Neaves is also bringing out a num
ber of greyhounds formerly owned by
Russell, Allen & Wilson. They are all
carefully selected and are expected to
prove high-class performers.
Jimmie Britt has been matched to fight
Perry Queenan in Portland early next
month. Queenan has defeated Dal Haw
kins and others recently.
William McCaffrey, the trainer, is com
ing West with Ralph Orthwein's kennel
of greyhounds from St. Louis. Sisquoc
and others are in the string. They will
remain until late in the year.
A meeting of the San Francisco Scot
tish Bowling Club will be held Friday
night in Scottish Hall to make arrange
ments for a series of games during the
coming season. It is expected the new
green in Golden Gate Park will be ready
for use in two months.
Sports Jottings.
Joe Carroll secured the decision over
Joe Hall after four rounds. Hall's game
ness was his only qualification.
Jerry Kenny and Perry Walker fur
nished the comedy element of the even
ing. Walker could not fight and ran
around the ring to avoid the attacks of
Kenny. Referee McDevitt stopped the
comedy in the third.
Lew Powell stopped Charles Augustus
in three rounds. The latter was down
eight seconds and his seconds threw up
the sponge to save him from punishment.
Pete Manning defeated Jimmy Little
after four desperate rounds. They ex
changed blow for blow throughout and
it seemed the fight would end any mo
ment. Manning was game and won in
the last few moments of the contest. '
Joe Reilly beat Ed Parker after one of
the greatest fights ever seen at one of
the minor clubs. Reilly proved a verit
able fighting machine and never gave
Parker a moment's rest. The fourth round
was all fighting, with Reilly doing the
most effective work. This -won him. the
decision. Â¦
The winner did the cleanest hitting in
the first, third and fourth rounds. He
set a whirlwind pace in the first and was
all over. Attell. The latter evened mat
ters up in the second round. Driscoll then
forced the fighting until the end, with At
tell always ready to mix matters.
Barney Driscoll was given the decision
over Monte Attell in a four-round boxing
bout last night in Mechanics' Pavilion
Annex. The judges -called it a draw. As
this is not permitted under the rules,
Referee McDevitt properly cast the de
ciding vote in favor of Driscoll.
Judges Call It a Tie, but the
Referee's Vote Decides
Contest.

RACES IN THE EAST.
In the race for the Prince of Wales
stake Ard Patrick, the Derby winner,
was a hot favorite at 2 to 1 on. The
horses finished as follows: The Duke of
Westminster's Cup Bearer (M. Cannon)
won; J. Gubbins' Ard Patrick (Martin),
second; Arthur James' Perseus (Maher),
third. Seven horses started. An objec
ti6n was lodged against Cup Bearer on
the ground of bumping and boring. The
stewards sustained the objection and Ard
Patrick, who was three-fourths of a
length behind, was awarded the race. The
Duke of Devonshire's Cheers, ridden by
Spencer, was placed third.
This cleared the way for the big race
of the day, the Ascot Stakes handicap of
twenty sovereigns each, which was won
by Scullion. Carabine finished second,
and Rambling Katie ran third. Thirteen
horses started.
LONDON, June 17.â The royal proces
sion left Windsor Castle for Ascot Heath
shortly before 1 p. m. Although King Ed
v/ard was unable to attend the races
Queen Alexandra, the Prince and Princess
of Wales, the Duke of Cambridge, the
Duke and Duchess of Connaught and the
other guests at the castle and members
of the court all participated. The proces
sion was the first full state parade of
that description since the. death of the
Prince Consort, and consisted .of eignt
carriages, each drawn by four horses.
The mounted equerries and gayly liveried
postilions and outriders formed an im
posing cavalcade. â¢.
Leaving the 1 palace quadrangle by the
George IV gate, the royal cortege drove
down the famous Long Walk, wiiich was
lined with cheering crowds, and, crossing
the review grounds, the procession reacn
ed the New Miles Gate and passed up
the course to' the royal pavilion.
In spite of the inclemency of the weath
er immense crowds assembled about the
course and . the royal party wa3 every
where warmly welcomed. The royal en
closure was brilliant with colors" and the
picturesque costumes of Indian princes.
A large number of special Krabas
sadors, colonial premiers and diplomats,
including Henry White, Secretary of the
UniteajStates Embassy, and Mrs. White;
John R. Carter, Second Secretary of the
Embassy, and Miss Carter and Miss
Choate were in attendance. Mesdames
Montgomery-Sears and Adair and
other Americans were also present
in the enclosure. United States Embas
sador Choate, Mrs. ' Choate, Whitelaw
Reid, the special Embassadorof the Unit
eu States, and Mrs. Reid did not go to
Ascot. All the big houses in and around
Ascot contributed their quota to the fash
icnable throng.
The gold vase of 200 sovereigns, given
by the King for three-year-olds, was won
by George Faber's Ice Maiden. Rock
Sand (Maher) won the Coventry Stake3.
The La Fleche filly (Martin) was second,
and Red Lily third.

Schooner Vine Arrives.
The schooner Vine, once Captain Burns'
3-acht. has completed another of her marvelous
voyages. Ehe left Salina Cruz 103 days ago for
Grays Harbor. Shs reached her destination
on Monday evening. The Vine is famous for
her leisurely gait and slender larder. Every
time ehe has made a clow passage it has been
a case of short rations for all hands. The
\-oyage from Salina Cruz to Grays Harbor
usually takes an industrious calling vessel from
thirty to forty days. The Vine left Salina Cruz
provisioned for a trip of two months. She was
more than three months on the way, and when
tbÂ« lighthouse tender Manzanita spoke her a
few days ago oft Cape Flattery ehe was not

for wheat and barley to Europe at 25s; old form
charter. v,

Advance of Kate.
The owners of the steamship Isls have agreed
to the terms submitted at Sandy Point for re
pairs of the damage done when the liner went
ashore a few days ago in the Straits of Magel
lan. The cost will be Â£4000, and it will take
nineteen days In which to do the work.
Repairs "Will Be Costly.
Major General Lloyd Wheaton and Brig
adier General Snyder are returning on
the Sherman, which is also bringing home
580 enlisted men of the Sixth Infantry,
568 enlisted men of the Nineteenth Infan
try, 51 sick, 11 insane and 42 discharged
men.
THE army transport officials are
preparing for a season of hard
work In the near future. They
are now engaged in getting the
Warren ready for sea, and before
they get through with her the transports
Kilpatrick, Hancock and Sherman will bo
inside the Golden Gate.
The Kilpatrick left Nagasaki May 29,
and is due here to-day. The Hancock left
Nagasaki June 3, and on June 4 the Sher
man sailed from the Japanese port. The
Hancock has on board 443 enlisted men of
the Ninth Infantry, probably the most
famous fighting organization in the serv
ice of the United States. The Ninth has
seen much fighting, has paid to the war
god a big tribute in brave lives and has
rendered invaluable service to the cause
of the administration in the Philippines.
In addition to this remnant of the Ninth
the Hancock is bringing fifty prisoners
and S14 casuals and recruits.
The United States revenue cutter Hugh Mc-
Culloch, Captain Coulsen commanding, sailed
yesterday for a practice cruise. She will go
to Magdalena Bay. where all her batteries will
be brought into commission and where officers
and crew will be Riven an opportunity to show
what they can do behind gun3 large and small.
McCulloch on Practice Cruise.
The schooner Newark, which arrived yester
day nine hours from Bowens Landing, made
the record trip for the run and beat the little
steamship Samoa, which left the landing ahead
of her by Just one hour. The Samoa is a fast
boat of her class and Captain Olsen helped out
his propeller with all the sail he could carry.
Each skipper got the best he could out of his
boat, but ten hours was the best in which the
Samoa could make the trip.

One of the Santa Fe Company's new
car transports was launched last evening
at the shipyards of Gratto & Rclmes in
South San Francisco. Many invited guests
were present. Promptly at 8 o'clock the
huge vessel slid down the ways as the
last block was knocked from under her.
Her dimensions are: Length over all 255
feet, breadth 36 feet and depth 12 feet.
She will carry sixteen cars and will ply
between this city and Point" Richmond;
Car Transport Launched.

Professor John TJri Lloyd of Cincinnati,
a well known authority on modern phar
macy and author of several stories of
Southern life, is at the Occidental hotel
accompanied by. his wife. He is touring
the coast and. expects to remain here a
Lloyd is the author of ; tho
scientific works. "The Chemistry of Medi
cines " "Elixirs and Their History" and
"Drugs and Medicines of North Amer
ica " He is also the author of "String
town on the ; Pike" and "Warwick of the
Knobs," two pieces of fiction. He was
raised in Kentucky and the former story
illustrates' the colored people and their
superstitions,' while the latter deals with
the "Rlggs" element.

While the Supervisors were in session
yesterday afternoon to award the bids for
the city's printing, Charles Marshall,,
representative of the H. S. Crocker Com
pany, one of the bidders for the contract,
and W. W. Copeland, representing tbe
Typographical Union, got into a discus
sion about the union labor question. Ccpe
land became much excited and rushed at
Marshall, shoving him from where he was
standing. James Bryan of the Typo-,
graphical Union came between the two
men . and prevented further trouble.'
Chairman Booth of the Supervisors ad
journed the. meeting until. order was re
stored, when the question w&s again taken
up. The board will pass upon the bids
for printing to-day. . .â¢ ,
The Pacific Coast Electric Transmission
Association opened its sixth annual con
vention yesterday in the maple room oÂ£
the Palace Hotel. Dr. Charles Van Nor
den of the Central California Electric
Company and president of the association,
after bidding the delegates welcome, de
livered his annual address, in which he
alluded to his prediction ' made . at
the convention held at Highgate
Falls, Vt.. that in a few years
from. that time the cataract of the neigh
borhood would in no short period furnish
light and power to nearby villages, none
nearer than nine miles. He then dis
cussed several problems which he con
sidered of vast importance.
r>r. Van Norden said In part:
The risk from labor troubles is not the least
of the factors In our equation. Linemen' and
station keepers have us at their mercy even
more so than motormen and conductors have
a city railroad. Already the linemen have
struck for the eight hour day and three dol
lar wage.-- Success may breed and encourage
larger demands. Allied to the growing menace
of organized labor is the seething of the com
munistic tendencies of the times. - Foremost
among the schemes urged 'is that of municipal
ownership of . public utilities. This we . need
not fear, since municipalities need our cheap
power. For us a far more dangerous agitation
is that which ..now proposes State appropria
tion of all water rights. Â¦ The scheme advocated
makes -the appropriation little less than sheer
confiscation. Indeed the movement is simply
an organization for public robbery on a large
scale. The agitation Is being vigorously pushed
in the name of progress, and the masses are
easily blinded as to their duties toward vested
rights and are often mislead Into extrava
gances. *
During the afternoon session the follow
ing papers were read:
''The Economy of Electric Power In ' Quarts
Mining," by C. O. Toole; 'The Application of
Electric Power In the Comstock Lode," by Leoo
M. Hall, and read -by George P. Low, and
"Electric Pumping From a 130O foot Level."
by Dcnald H. Fry.
In the evening the delegates listened to
studious. and eminently scientific papers
and also enjoyed stereoptlcon views pre
sented by Professor Alexander McAdle.
The professor announced that two weeks
ago last Sunday this city did itself proud
inasmuch as it beat all known records in
the velocity of the wind. On that day
the wind blew, at a rate not less than 125
miles an hour at Point Lobos, which was
a much higher velocity than attained at
Galvcston at. the time of the memorable
disaster. "So as a windy city," said Pro
fessor. McAdie, "San Francisco passes
Chicago or Boston.'.' Hia paper was en
titled "Cloud Versus Coal."
A. J. Bowie. Jr. read a paper entitled
"Wells in Large Ranges." "The Hy
draulic End of Power Transmissions"
was the title of a paper by John S. East
wood.
E. J. de. Sabla Jr. was elected president
for the ensuing year: H. H. Noble of the
Keswlck Electric Power Company vice
president: William Angus of the Subur
ban Electric Company. Oakland, was re
elected treasurer, and George P. Low of
the Journal of Electricity. Power and Gin
of San Francisco was re-elected secre
tary. .- - . .'
â¢This morning other papers will be read,
and in the afternoon vÂ«s;ts will be made
to the electric and gas plants at Oakland
by the delegates.. -.
DEMANDS DAMAGES.â Mabel Brooks, who

Gustav Saulr, who claims he belongs to
the Seventh Infantry, was arrested yes
terday by Detective Reynolds and Police
man Collins and locked up in "the tanks"
pending an investigation. He visited a
money broker on Kearny street, near Sac
ramento, and asked to have a draft for
$90, drawn on the First National Bank
of Portland, cashed. The broker was sus
picious of the draft and notified the
police.
An examination showed that the draft
had originally been for $10 and had been
clumsily raised to $30. Across the draft
the words. "Not over ninety dollare,"
were stamped, but It could be seen that
the original stamp was "Not over ten
dollars."
When searched at the City Prison an
other draft, raised from $10 to $90. one
from. $10 to $50 and a genuine draft for
$25, all dated April 5 last, were found in
his possession.

Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
â¢ * 1118 Broadway. Juno 17. .
It is a peculiar fact that for the past
six years many more Chinese have been
landed, at- this port than Japanese, not
withstanding that there is an exclusion
law against Chinese and . none against
Japanese. . ;
There is no law prohibiting the contract
ing of laborers within the United States,
and : Hawaii -being a part of the United
States, is considered as a coastwise do
mestic port; therefore there is nothing to
hinder the entire Japanese population of
the islands from coming to this country
under labor contracts. . .
Captain Schell explained, further that
there is a popular misconception regard
ing Japanese immigrants. The truth is
that the Japanese immigrant stands on
the same footing as Irish, French, Ger
man or English immigrants, and there is
no exclusion law against them.
These people will be allowed' to enter. More
than that, their right to enter cannot be ques
tioned. We have no right to examine or In
terfere with them at all, no more than we have
to examine white persons coming from Missouri
or Oregon under contract to labor In California.
Hawaii Is a part of the United States, and we
do not board steamers coming from Honolulu or
Seattle.- We might as well interfere with Sac
ramento Valley farmers contracting with labor
ers in San Francisco.
Deputy Immigrant Commissioner Schell,
in speaking of the matter yesterday, said:
All of these coolies are coming under con
tract in violation of the Immigration laws
which strictly forbid the admission of contract
laborers. If these people are allowed to en
ter It will be. through some subterfuge devised
and adopted for the evasion of the immigration
laws.
Much amusement was created in the
United States Immigration Bureau over
an editorial in the morning issue of a
daily paper calling attention to the fact
that Japanese laborers under contract to
work in California were arriving and pre
paring to arrive at this port from Ha
waiian Territory. The editorial spates:
After but little more than a year of
married life, Augusta Thill has brought
a suit for divorce. She alleges that Nicho
las Thill is so addicted to the use of In
toxicants that to continue living with him
as his wife is more than she can bear.
According to her complaint he commenced
drinking heavily a short time after their
marriage at Oakland in March, 1901, with
the result that he is unable to attend to
business and earn enough for their joint
support. Thill, alleges his wife, has
beaten her frequently when in his cups.
She asks for a divorce and permission to
resume her maiden name.

"The message which we received from
Santa Barbara this morning shows that a
new quarter Is affected by the grasshop
pers. It looks as if they would soon be all
over the farming districts of the State.
We hear that at Exeter, in Tulare Cour.
tv, they have the pest under control, but
in Placer, El Dorado and Sacramento
counties the measures taken for the sup
pression of the insects are entirely inade
quate and they are on the increase."
BERKELEY, June 17.â Professor E. W.
Hilgard, dean of the College of Agricul
ture at the University of California, re
ceived an appeal for aid from Santa Bar
bara to-day against the grasshopper pest.
This is the first indication that the army
of hoppers has moved away from, the
foothill region of the Interior, .and is taken
by Professor Hilgard as significant of the
fact that the plague which has heretofore
been confined to one or two districts will
probably become general throughout the
agricultural districts of the State.
Professor Hilgard takes a serious view
of the spread of the pest. He said:
Grasshopper Pest.
Santa Barbara Suffering From the
PLAGUE IS SPREADING.

STATE COMMITTEE.
Third â A constitutional amendment making
provision for the free, due and speedy admin
istration of Justice in courts, into which the
people, rich and poor alike, may freely enter
and have their cases beard* and determined
without the prepayment of costs of any kind,
also prohibiting the use of Injunctions and
other summary process in matters affecting
the reJiition of employer and employe.
Fourth â That as no enduring: prosperity can
come to the agricultural districts of the State
without the complete utilization of the Irri
gation waters of the mountains, we demand
their ownership and distribution by the State,
and further, that the State establish a land
system under the "New Zealand plan," that
congestion of population in the cities may be
relieved and easy access to farms be accom
plished; and further, that street co-operative
laws bÂ« enacted, giving producers the means
of uniting for the more equitable distribution
of the* products of factory and field, all for
the upbuilding of the State.
St-cond â A- constitutional amendment abro
gating the laws and provisions of the consti
tution authorizing and permitting any fur
ther extension of the private ownership of any
of. the public utilities of the State or of its
counties or municipalities and making full
and adequate provision for the acquisition,

CHAIRMAN READS REPORT.
The Industrial Federation party, now in con
vention assembled, calls upon the^ people of
California to join one supreme and resistless
eifort to rescue the State from the clutches of
monopoly nnd bossism. toj stamp out corrup
tion in Its government and to upbuild the
State, to accomplish the end we demand:
While it caused some delay, the report
as read by the chairman ot the commit
tee, D. T. Fowler, was eventually adop
ted as a whole and was as follows:
In fact there would not have been any
unpleasantness if J. G. Smith of Alameda
County had not tried to wedge in a sub
stitute for the first section of the report
of the Committee on Platform and Reso
lutions.
The evening session showed less inclina
tion to wrangle and more to get through
with the business before it.
WRANGLING DIMINISHES.
A motion to strike from the roll the
name of any person- who was pledged to
the Socialist. Democratic or Republican
parties was carried by a vote of 29 to 9.
The report. of the committee on creden
tials was adopted and the chair was em
powered to fill the vacancies on the com
mittee on platform and resolutions. An
amendment was carried that the commit
tee should consist of seven members . in
stead of twelve, which were chosen as
follows: George "W. Monteith, F. B.
Brown, W. H. Gllstrefp, W. G. Murphy,
D. T. Fowler, W. H. Buckley and J. K.
Riddell. The committee then went into
executive session and the convention ad
journed at 5:30 p. m.

A motion was made and carried that a
new committee on credentials, composed
of W. J. Gilstrap, M. P. Squire and J. L.
Riddell, be appointed.
It was then seen clearly that the social
ists were attempting to block proceedings
and swallow the convention, and after
three hours of heated debate, with innu
merable ' interruptions, a motion was at
last carried to accept the report of the
committee on credentials and then dis
charge the committee.
SOCIALISTS INTERRUPT.
So far everything had moved smoothly,
but trouble came. The committee on plat
form and resolutions elected by the com
mittee on credentials could not come to
any agreement and stood 6 to 6.
The State Convention of the Allied Peo
ple's party was called to order yesterday
at 10:50 a. m. in Memorial Hall, Odd Fel
lows' building, by George W. Monteith,
chairman of the State Executive Com
mittee.
Mr. Monteith welcomed the delegates
and on behalf of the committee presented
the name, of Dr. C. H. Castle as tempo
rary . chairman. Dr. Castle was elected,
and G. W. Smith was elected temporary
secretary.
A committee on credentials was then
chosen, which in its turn elected a com
mittee on platform and resolutions, com
posed of twelve members.
During the morning the committee on
permanent organization was chosen, as
follows: C. J. Gardner, Nathan E. Flint,
M. P. Squire, John Hines and Charles E.
Lloyd. The morning session adjourned at
12 o'clock and the committee on platform
and resolutions went into session.
The afternoon, session opened with the
adoption of the report of the committee
on permanent organization. This com
mittee recommended that the name of the
party be "The Industrial Federation."'
The temporary officers were made perma
nent and J." K. Phillips was elected treas
urer by acclamation and F. J. Gregory
sergeant at arms.
The directors of the Xorth Shore Rail
road, formerly the JCorth Pacific Coast
Railway, made the announcement yester
day that 5750,000 wcrth of the 5 per cent
forty-year gold bonds had been sold. The
bonds were sold through the Mercantile
Trust Company of this city at par. ;
The directors now intend to commence
vork on the improvements they an
nounced they had in view. A new and
commodious ferry-boat â¢will be built and
placed in commission. The tracks to iJill
Valley and San Rafael, from SausalHo.
will be made standard gauge and 70-pound
rails used. These two roads will be
changed into electric systems, the rolling
stock having been already ordered.
The rotd from San Anselmo to Caza
dcro will also be overhauled and balanced
â¢with gravel, and heavier rails laid. The
officials of the company feel assured that
the improvements they are about to make
will do much to increase the population of
Marin County.
"I have wired to Sacramento asking tor
more definite information as to the situa
tion In Placer and EH Dorado counties,
â¢where It is said grasshoppers have become
a pest. Until I am informed as to the ex
act situation, I cannot say what steps will
be taken in regard to the request to use
the authority vested in me to order the
burning of pasture lands in thosd coun
ties. I shall probably be advised to-mor
row, when whatever action' is necessary
will be taken."
LOS ANGELES, June 17.â Governor
Gage said to-night in regard to a tele
gram sent to him by Chairman Dillman of
the Sacramento Board of Supervisors, con
veying the opinion of Attorney General
Ford on the subject of burning pasture
lands in El Dorado and Placer counties:
Referring to the Becond branch of your in
quiry, I beg to say that the State Board ot
Horticulture, beinc a creature of the statute, is
clothed with no sovereign powers, but is limited
to the powers specifically conferred upon it by
statute or those necessarily implied from the
statutory powers thus specifically jcranted.
I find no statutory authority which would
warrant your board in taking any of the sug
gested steps to arrest the threatened calamity
?tÂ» which ytfu refer, such as the burning ot
p&Eture lands and the like, nor do I rind the
statute of this State conferring: uoon any
officer, Mjeciflcally, the cower* herein dis
cussed. It follows, therefore, that this power
of the State can alone be exercised by its
Chief Executive, in whom, under the provis
ions of our State constitution, is vested the
supreme executive power of the State.
Of course the facts must warrant the exer
cise of this unusual and extraordinary power
of the State. According to the authorities, the
necessity therefor must be "^extreme, impera
tive and overwhelming." Whether the facts
to which you refer would warrant the exercise
of the power above iioted is a question upon
which I can. of course, express no opinion. This
question being one of fact must be determined
by those who represent the State in the exer
cise o* the above-described uower. If the
threatened calamity to which you refer is im
minent, grave and farreaching in its results;
it it affect a considerable section ot the State,
involving several counties therein; if the burn
ing over of certain pasture lands will prevent
the threatened calamity; and if by no other
means the threatened calamity can be averted,
then in my judgment, a condition has arisen
which would warrant the State in making use
or' the inherent and sovereign power abors
noted.
In reply to your first inquiry I beg to say
that the State of California in its sovereign
capacity is clothed with inherent power to pro
tect and preserve its own existence and to do
al! things needful to prevent or avert ji threat
ened calamity involving widespread destruction
of persons or property. This power is based in
part upon the old and well-known principle
that private right is and always must be sub
servient to the public good. In exercising the
power thus inherently belonging to the sov
ereign, the State of California may, where It
becomes necessary, take,- use or destroy the
private property of individuals' to prevent the
impending calamity, as, tor instance, to stop
the spreading of fire, to stay the ravages of
a pestilence, to arrest tjie advance of a hostile
army or to prevent any other great public
calamity. This power of the State to. act in
cases of great public necessity does not require
statutory authority, but comes directly from
the natural law, from the right of self-preser
vation.

from destruction by grasshoppers and

by the State Board of Horticulture in
connection with this matter, rendered tn-?

The House spent the day considering
bills reported from the Judiciary Com
mittee. By far the most important meas
ure was that to amend the existing bank
ruptcy law. The minority made a vigor
ous effort to repeal the present law in
toto, but was overwhelmingly defeated, 65
to 137. The bill passed amends the law
In fifteen particulars to meet defects
which It is said experience has proven.
The most important amendment is one
to define preference, to meet the Supreme
Court decision in the case of Pirie vs the
Chicago Title and Trust Company. Four
additional grounds for refusing a dis
charge in bankruptcy also are added:
First, obtaining property on credit on ma
terially false statements: second, making
a fraudulent transfer of property; third
having been granted or denied a discharge
in bankruptcy within six years, and
fourth, having refused to obey the order
Â©f the court or refusal to answer to ma
.terial questions approved by the court.

It wilL be a sad day for our nation when
the forces of education and the teachers, of
moral living shall Â¦ cease to strive In unity to
leaven the entire mass of our citizenship or
wherr their 'influence 'in" that "direction 'shall '- : be
- This it seems to me is impressively illus
trated when the severely Catholic College of
St. Thomas- of "Villanova bestows its highest
honorary degree upon one connected with tha
management and holding an honorary degree
in the severely Protestant Princeton University.
The processes of education, as they exist In
this country of ours, have, or always should
Have, in addition to other characteristics, es
pecial harmony of purpose and design, as they
are related to our Government, and this
should constitute between our Institutions of
learning a bond of close fraternity. Whatever
other objects and purposes may be involved in
educational efforts among us, one of Its con
stant and prominent aims should be the culti
vation and maintenance of, a high standard of
American citizenship. When we recall the fact
that the beneficence of our scheme of govern
ment depends upon the virtue of education of
units of our citizenship, it is at once apparent
that an important and common duty rests upon
every agency that undertakes tha instruction
of the youth of our land. Â¦,

mind the Imperious edict of education, which
forbids the hindrance or disturbance of lta high

tolerance or. any â¢ of the barriers tha,t to a.
greater or less extent separate civilized hu
manity. The republic of education ia based

tunity and impartiality In the distribution of
its rewards and honors.

HANDSOME TODNG WOMAN
WHO MARRIED 'A CITIZEN
OF SCOTLAND.
Bribing voters by the loan of oxen is a new form
of corrujition, and has the merit of being economical.
It will be seen that even when on victory bent the
Vermont politician retains a thrifty mind. -
Charges r and counter charges of bribery at the
primaries fill the Vermont air. Among the affi
davits published on the subject is one that runs thus:
"I, S. Deno,. 73 years of age, a legal voter in the town
of Shoreham, county of Addison, State of Vermont,
duly 'Jo depose* an^Ssay pn oath that I was offered the
use of a pair of oxen for two months by M. Mack,
one of J. G. McCyllough's managers, to cast my ballot
at the Republican caucus held at Shoreham, jVt., on
May 31, 1902, for the McCullough candidates to the
State convention to be held at Montpelier, Vt., on
June IV, 1902." â¢ ; . â¢ .
Whatever may be the cause of the trouble there is
no denying the existence of the trouble itself. At the
primaries the neutral candidate is said to have ob
tained the election of 312 delegates to the State con
vention, the prohibitionists 169 and the local option
man 222. There are twenty-five other votes, not
pledged, and the convention, which assembles on
Thursday, is expected to be red-hot and full of
ginger.
There are- various theories. concerning the cause of
the outbreak. Three rich men are candidates for the
Republican nomination for Governor, and some say
their money has done the talking that has caused all
the excitement. One faction stands for prohibition,
another for high license and local option, while the
third is neutral; so some folks say whisky is at the
bottom of the row. Finally, one candidate represents
the Central Vermont -Railway, another the Rutland
Railway, while the third represents no railway; and
accordingly the inference is drawn that the ruction is
nothing more nor less than a large and general rail
way collision.
FOR many a year past politics in Vermont has
been safe, quiet and one-sided. So much of a
foregone conclusion has each successive election
been that flippant people in other States have spoken
of a r Republican nomination in Vermont as a sure
thing, a pudding, a snap and a cinch. This year the
unexpected has happened.' The long dormant volca
noes of political, strife have broken out in a Pelee
eruption. There is more politics to the acre in Ver
mont this year ; than in New York, Pennsylvania,
Ohio, or even, Indiana. So great has been the up
roar that all JJew England has become excited, and
even in Washington political experts are neglecting
the affairs of the nation to talk of Vermont.
Beginning the new era in their history with such
a spirit, the Boers are almost certain to achieve in
the end the high purpose at which they aimed. Men
who can meet dangers so bravely and bear disaster
with such fortitude may be .overcome by resistless
numbers, but they can never be completely" con^
quered. Meeting their former foes without malice
the Boers will face the new conditions .of their
country and prove themselves the dominant element
of its population/so that in the end the federation of
South Africa will be essentially a -Boer republic
animated by the'Boer spirit.
Were Dewet the only eminent leader of the burgh
ers who lias spoken in that way the utterance might
be supposed to be the expression of feelings peculiar
to himself. However, he is not the only one. All
other notable representative men of Boers have said
substantially, the same thing. For example, General
Botha and Acting President Schalkburger of the
Transvaal in an address to their people after the sur
render said: ."Now, that there is peace, and although
it is not a peace such as we longed for, yet let us
abide where God has led us. We can, with a clear
conscience, declare that for two and a half years pur
people have carried on the struggle in a mariner al
most unknown to history. Let us now grasp each
other's hands, for another great struggle lies before
us _ a struggle for the spiritual and racial prosperity
and welfare of our people. Casting aside all feelings
of bitterness, let us learn to forget and to forgive, so
that the deep wounds caused by this war may be
healed "
Speaking- to the Boers in the prison camp at Win
burg General Dewet, after acivising them to be loyal
to the Britith Government, said: "Perhaps it is hard
for you to hear this from- my mouth, but God has
decided thus. I fought until there was no more hope
cf upholding our cause, and, however it may be, the
lime has now come to lay down our arms. As a
Christian, God now demands that we be faithful to
our new Government. Let us submit to his deci
sion.",, . .
There- is no inconsistency between the vigorous
prosecution of what threatened to become a war of
extermination and the frank acceptance of peace. In
each case the spirit is the same â that of an intense
religious fervor, a firm fixed belief that the destiny
of mortals is decreed by the 4 will of God; that when
a man has done his best, heroically, but failed, it
then becomes his duty to submit with fortitude to the
ways of Providence.
:WeVe this spirit of loyalty accompanied by a feel
ing of hatred for the victor and a desire for revenge
the association would seem natural. In fact, when
the war was raging it was believed that a people so
stern as the Boers would never be reconciled to
British rule even were it established over their coun
try by irresistible force. No such feeling, however,
has been manifest since the terms of I peace were
agreed upon. On the contrary, there has been a
remarkable display of good will on both sides.
Kitchener's speeches to the Boers have been suffi
ciently noble and generous in sentiment to be termed
magnanimous, while those of the Boer leaders have
shown in ihe acceptance of defeat a spirit as lofty as
that which prompted them in their hours of bright
est hope.
GENERAL DEWET visited the prison camp
at Winburg last Friday and was received by
the Boers with an extraordinary manifesta
tion of loyalty and affection. The men cheered him
and the women and children were moved to tears
in their efforts to express their love for their hero.
Kitchener, the victor of the war, will never receive
such a welcome as was accorded by the conquered
burghers to their defeated hero. It was another
manifestation of the indomitable courage of the
Boer people. In defeat as in victory they remain true
to their leaders and their cause.

. the progress of the country. If we have free trade as
a principle we can at least figure on the future and c it
our cost according to our cloth
industry so uncertain as to injure them and hinder
tween this country and the islands.
Among the many valid objections to that annexa
' tion was the condition there that makes Asiatic coolie
fcbor absolutely necessary to the profit of the planta
tions and the prosperity of the islands. This objec
tion was frequently and" forcibly made by The Call,
End its correctness has been cbnfessed by the an
nexation party on the islands, who plead for a dif
ferential policy as to coolie labor that will exclude
it from the continent but permit it in the archipelago.
Cuba has a large laboring population of tropical ne
groes and has less need for Asiatic coolies. Long
before the annexation of Hawaii we controlled more
than 90 per cent of her trade, so that no commercial
perturbation could possibly follow the political ab
sorption of that country. , "
As the President is retrospective others may in
dulge in that method of argument When the Ding
ley tariff passed the sugar rate was framed to protect
American cane and beet sugar. Dingley, Dalzell and
Payne so declared, and put that feature before the
farmers of the country as the highest merit of the
measure affecting the interests of agriculture. Ding.
ley is dead, but Payne and Dalzell are living and still
in their' seats in the House. What they said to cheer
the farmers on for the Dingley tariff has been
quoted to them during the debate on Cuban free
trade, and they sat silent.
Suppose now, to be retrospective again, that when
we responded to the cry of Cuba in 1898 she had
asked that we not only give ler freedom at our ex
pense but that we also give lfcr free trade, does any
one believe that we would have responded to the
mercenary request? Her appeal was sentimental en
tirely, and in those days, when we sympathized with
her aspiration for freedom to the extent of violating
the neutrality .laws and our treaties with Spain to aid
4 her -in rebellion long before our formal declaration
of war, no sordid motive mingled with her appeal for
help. If such motive had been suggested, would our
response have met with .such general approval? Let
the President answer that question.
Retrospection 'confirms the declaration that not a
shot would have been fired in behalf of Cuba'had the
commercial motive been whispered. This being true,
the attack upon protection is an afterthought en
tirety. It has influenced our Cuban policy since the
liberation only, and not before. In our view it
threatens the whole policy of protection. If it be true
that we need the thirty millions of Cuban trade, it is
I still truer that we need the many hundred millions of
trade of the British Isles, and if we adopt free trade
to get the small trade of Cuba, what answer can the
President make if free trade is said to be necessary
to hold our commerce with Great Britain?
If protection is a policy of expediency and not of
principle it is far better to abolish it entirely, because
when treated as expediency it makes investment and
ignorance of the trade relations long established be-
PRESIDENT ROOSEVELT has entered into
the cause of Cuban free trade with his usual
zeal, earnestness and frankness. Congress and
the country have the felicity of knowing just where
he stands. In his special message on that subject he
indulges in one faulty retrospection, to which atten
tion may be called. He draws an analogy between
Cuban free trade and the opposition thereto and the
objections that were made to the annexation of
Hawaii. In that connection he says: "Some of our
citizens oppose the lowering of the tariff on Cuban
products just as three years ago they opposed the
admission of the Hawaiian Islands lest free trade
with them might ruin certain of our interests here.
In the actual event their fears proved baseless as re
gards Hawaii, and their apprehensions as to the
carnage to any industry of our own because of the
proposed measure of reciprocity with Cuba seem to
be equally baseless.*'
While it is true that such an objection to the an
nexation of Hawaii may have been made in some
quarters, it weighed so little in the general mass of
opposition that but few can recall it. As a matter of
fact Hawaii, for more than twenty years, had free
trade with this country in all her staple products and
free exchange with sll our staples necessary to the
life of her people. So, if such objection as * the
President recalls were made, it originated in entire
CUBA AND HAWAII.
It will be noted that the Prison Directors have set a day for beginning an investi
gation of the same matters that are involved in the Governor's suit for betterment of
his reputation. They will need the same witnesses; the same books, accounts and records
and may need them at the same time that they are under subpena at San Pedro. Does
the Governor desire to prevent investigation by the Prison Directors? It is quite cer
tain that a judicial inquiry, going' on at San Pedro and an administrative investigation
going on at the same time at San Quentin will not serve the ends of justice, nor tend to
develop the facts. It may be that responsibility for a proper trial and an. unembarrassed
inquiry will be thrown upon the defendants, and, if so," they will not hesitate to assume
it, for they have no wish to lie down upon the taxpayers, as the Governor has done, nor
to delay nor hinder the consideration of the mass of evidence in the case. , '
A man who is in the pangs of longing for vindication, who is hectic with a
febrile desire to appear whiter than snow before the State Convention occurs, should
have sought more convenient means and a handier locality in which to give his reputauon
a bath. We believe there is a Justice of the Peace at Indio, in the Colorado Desert, below
the level of the sea. Why did not the Governor go to Indio? What does he fear in San
Francisco, Sacramento or even in Los Angeles? If he is all right, and can disprove all
pleas of justification made by the defendants, his disproof is just as good in sight of San
Quentin and the scene of the charges as out of sight, in far away San. Pedro. If he desire
speedy justice why go so far for a forum that justice will get footsore plodding to the trial?
â¢ The Governor is evidently determined to restore the gaudy glow of his good
name, regardless of the cost to the people. Just why he chose San Pedro is not dis
closed. That quiet marine town has not heretofore established a reputation as a libel
trial resort. It had a somewhat stormy birth as a seaport, and was favored by Congress
because of its obvious advantages as the port of Los Angeles, and - there seems to
be nothing in its past, present or future to recommend it aÂ» a sanitarium for damaged
reputations, or a receiving hospital for good names that have been sprained in other
parts of the State. In fact its sole adaptability to the Governor's uses seem to be its ex
treme distance from the scene of the accident which he, complains has befallen
his character. /
It is nearly five hundred miles from San Quentin to San Pedro. In a proceeding
for criminal libel the people have to pay the expenses of the actual plaintiff, who in this
case is the Governor of California. An officer has to come with the warrant, handcuffs' and
mileage, to drag the trembling prisoners far from home and friends', and is entitled to col
lect, for himself and them, railroad fare and .seventy-five cents a meal, eh route. The
books and accounts of the prison, records in the Controller's office, if they have got
back from' the Governor's private inquiry, and the witnesses, including officers of the
prison and prisoners in, their charge, all must be taken to San Pedro and returned in
good repair, at the public expense.

TO PRESERVE
THE LANDMARKS
OF CALIFORNIA
The following resolutions were adopted
by the Grand parlor cf the Native Daugh
ters on the subject of the preservation of
historical landmarks In the State of Cali
fornia:
"Whereas. The order of the Native Daughters
of the Golden West has already signified ft*
desire to enter upon the work outlined In tha
Reichling resolution, and
"Whereas. The historical landmarks commit
tee appointed to act under â¢ the Relchling reso
lution has presented a full report and offered
suggestions for the consideration of the Grand
Parlor; be it ' , -
Resolved. That this Grand Parlor, here as
sembled. Indorses the aims and objects of the
California Historical Landmarks League and
of the Landmarks Club cf Southern California,
and urges Native Daughters, as Individuals,
to Join either of these associations; and be it
Resolved. That the Grand Parlor indorses the
worÂ» of the Pasadena Exhibit Association in
undertaking to restore El Carmel real, anj
make it tfie grand highway of the State; an I
be It '
Resolved, That the Grand Parlor Invites the
subordinate parlors to attempt to secure tha
co-operation ot their local Boards of Super
visors toward restoring that portion of tha
road passing through their respective counties;
and be It
Resolved. That this Grand Parlor indorses
the suggestions for the study of California his
tory and of the institution of a chair of Cali
fornia history in the University of California;^
and be it
Resolved, That this Grand Parlor appropriate
the sum of $50 toward purchasing books on
California topics for the Grand Parlor library
to be kept In the grand secretary'* office, the
money to be expended by the historical land
marks committee In consultation with the grand
president; and be it further
Resolved. That this Grand Parlor indorses the
committee's suggestions regarding the study of
forestry and planting of memorial trees, tha
preservation of cur wild flowers from extinc
tion and the extension of the area of our forest
reservations. %
ELIZA D. KEITH. Chairman,
LILT O. REICHLING DYER,
MINNIE W. HILKE,
Historical Landmarks Committee, N. D. G. W.
Grand President Miss Keith will, at tha
same time that she sends out her
circular address to the subordinate
parlors of the State, send a copy
of the resolutions. 1 to each. It Is tho
purpose of the officers of the Grand Par
lor to carry out the suggestion made by a
pioneer of this city to one of the. past
grand presidents about four years ago
that the Native Daughters and the Native
Sons work to the end that all kinds of his
torical facts be collated by these organi
zations for the purpose of preparing a full
history of the State and of San Francisco.

Peculating Clerk Is Sentenced
Bids for repairing the transport War
ren were received by Major Devol, gen
eral superintendent "of the transport ser
vice; yesterday, and the contracts were
awarded to the Union Iron Works and
the Western Repair and Supply Company.
The" Union Iron Works gets the con
tract \ for repairing the engines, boilers
and hull for $3640, the electrical work for
$310 and the carpenter and joiner work
for $184. The Western Repair, and Supply
Company gets the plumbing and gallery
work for $190. . -,r -w
Other bidders were the Fulton Iron
Works, Risdon-Iron Works and Mare
Island Navy Yard. The contract awarded
in each case was to the lowest bidder.

ply Company.

James E. Fields, one of the senators
from this State and doorkeeper of the
senate, left for the East yesterday, and
will in due time reach Niagara Falls.
Davis Louderback of Union Council was
chosen president and E. H. Hills of San
Francisco Council secretary. - Norman R
Arter of Golden Gate Council was placed
in nomination by W. A. S. Nicholson of
California Council,. Henry "W. Maas of
Pacific Council was nominated by J. H.
Cutter of the same council and Dr. A.
H. Mlllberry of San Francisco Council
by E. H. Hills. .
Oh the first ballot Maas received a ma
jority of the votes and on motion of Dr
Millberry, seconded by Arter, the election
was made unanimous.
The California Assembly of the Na
tional Union held a meeting in Red Men's
Hall yesterday afternoon for the purpose

to meet at Niagara Falls on July 15, next.

A grand v night parade will probably be
the feature v of the coming Fourth of July
celebration. Market, Kearny and Mont
gomery streets will be lighted as they
were during the recent, visit of the
Shriners, and many of the principal build
ings that were illuminated on that occa
sion will add to the attraction of the pa
geant by their electric adornments.
At a meeting of the committee held
yesterday afternoon at the California
Hotel the matter of holding a parade was
discussed, and met- with general fa
vor. ,. The only obstacle in the way,
however, is the word of Colonel J. B.
Rawles that the troops from the Presidio
cannot parade at night. >A* large number
of merchants have expressed a determi
antionto place handsome floats in the
line of march, and with the National
Guard and civic organizations the absence
of the soldiers may not' be greatly noted.
Fireworks will be exploded along the line
of march, and the entire affair promises
to be one of the most brilliant of its kind
ever held in the city. - Â¦ ,. .
Owing to the additional cost of lighting
Market, Kearny and Montgomery streets
on the night of the Fourth of July it was
decided to solicit subscriptions to the
amount of $000, so that the allotment for
fireworks will not have to be curtailed;
An effort will be made to have a man-of
war artistically illuminated in the harbor.
A petition will be presented to the Gov
ernor of the State asking him to declare
Saturday, July 5, a legal holiday, and the
various religious denominations will . be
requested to embody patriotic exercises in
their programmes on Sunday. July 6. The
proposed regatta. on the afternoon of the
Fourth was abandoned. Exercises will
be held at Metropolitan Temple. Attor
ney George A. Knight will be asked to
deliver the oration. Miss Etta Welch will
5ing the ."Star-spangled Banner," Charles
R. Bonestell willread the Declaration of
Independence, ; Rev. A. C. Bane will de
liver an invocation,. Rev. 'Father Wy
man the benediction, and the Knicker
bocker Quartet' will sing patriotic selec
tions. Â¦ Â¦ ' .. â¢
The committee will meet again Friday
afternoon at 4 o'clock.

She Will Have the Man
agement of Vast
Estate.
Mrs. Sarah Sloss, widow of Louis Sloss,
the California pioneer, who died at San
Rafael on the 4th inst., will inherit her
late husband's vast estate. In his will,
which will be filed within a few days, the
deceased directs that all his possessions
shall go to his devoted wife. He makes
this disposition of his property, he states
in the document, because he feels con
fident that before she is called to her last
rest she will make a just and equitable di
vision of the property among their five
children, Leon, Louis Jr., Superior Judge
M. C. a.nd Joseph Sloss and Mrs. E. R.
Lilienthal. V ,
Sloss also leaves a number of bequests
to charitable organizations. The Pacific
Hebrew Orphan Asylum will receive $10,
000, the Roman Catholic and the Protest
ant Orphan Asylums $2500 each, the Chil
dren's Hospital J2500 and the Golden Gate
Kindergarten Association $2500.

The local aerie has been making exten
sive preparations for its trip to Napa to
attend the grand celebration to be given
by the Eagles of that place on July 4. It
has chartered a steamboat to convey its
members to the festivities and they in
tend to drop in on their country brothers
in bright uniforms and regalia.
It is to help pay the expenses of the trip
that the minstrel entertainment has been
arranged.

Dies From His Wound.
v Emmet de Suza, who was shot by hia
stepfather, Joseph Guadalupe, at 1329
Fourteenth avenue, Sunday, died at the
City and County Hospital early yesterday
morning- and his body was removed to
the Morgue. Guadalupe is still detained
in the City Prison, but no charge will be
placed against him pending the result of
the Coroner's inquest.
Sues Rival for Damages.
Louis Epstein, who keeps a second-hand
store on Mission street, filed a suit yester
day to recover $5000 damages from Henry
Sparber, a business rival. He charges
Sparber with assaulting him last Satur
day. He further alleges that during the
scuffle that ensued 3155 in coin was lost
from his pocket.
Another significant change, indicating
the blowing of the wind, is the engage
ment of Professor H. Morse Stephens of
Cornell University as professor of the de
partment of modern history- He has been
put- over the head of Professor Thomas
Bacon, and has also been given charge of
the university extension work. Bacon
has been at Berkeley for some years
Instructor Archie Pierce of the depart
ment of mathematics is also said to be
s.&ted for removal. He was given a
year's leave of absence in 1901, and it -Is
said he will not return. -
President Wheeler is now in the south
ern part of the State, but he will return
to attend the meeting of the regents in
July. There are many of the instructors
who would like to have a look at the list
of the faculty that President Wheeler
will prepare for submission to the regents
Gossip also says that Professor Albin
O. Putzker *>f the German department is
slated for removal. PuUker was reduced
last year from the head of the department
and was given charge of the instruction
of German literature. His salary was
also reduced. Professor Schilling was
brought from Yale College to replace
Putzger.
Professor L. Du Pont Syle. associate
professor of English, is also likely to be
dispensed with. Syle is now in Europe
and will return next month. He was
raised from the rank of an assistant pro
fessor to that of associate professor twa
years ago, despite the opposition of Pro
fessor Gayley, the head of the depart
ment. Rumor now says that Gayley has
won the ear of the "powers that be'-' and
that Syle will be ousted.
Rumor has it that Professor Christy,
the head of the mining department, will
be replaced by an Eastern man. Profes
sor Fred Slate of the department Of
physics is said to be another name on the
"decapitation list."
Captain Emil Kellner, for many years
superintendent of the university grounds,
has been replaced by Walter Bolton, 23
years of age. Kellner has been reduced
to the rank of .head gardener of the de
partment of agriculture, while his suc
cessor is said to have come to Berkeley
"direct from a ranch in Arizona."
A similar state of things exists in the
case of John A. Reid, an instructor in
the department of geology. He will not
apply lor reappointment at the next me'et
ing of the regents.
Lorin Hunt, an instructor of the depart
ment of civil engineering, and Walter
Chandler, who held a similar post, have
asked President Wheeler not to present
their names to the regents for reappoint
ment. Both gentlemen have secured other
positions, and rumor says that they were
"informed officially" that they stood no
chance of re-election.
Instructor Shinn, inspector of agricul
tural stations, resigned last month. He
stated, however, that his resignation was
forced, and considerable feeling was en
gendered. , - .. , .
For some time past rumors have filled
the 'air that President Benjamin Ide
Wheeler proposes to replace many of the
instructors with Eastern men and that
his views are fully indorsed by the re
gents.
There are some people* living in the uni
versity town who say' that President
Wheeler controls the regents, and that
when a well known attorney was recently
appointed to the board he asked a fellow
member for some information as to pro
cedure and he was told, "Wait until Pres
ident Wheeler nods and then vote 'yes'."
A number of changes in the faculty* at
Berkeley University made recently give
color tos the rumors of wholesale decapita
tion of instructors.
The July meeting of the Board of Re
gents of the State University is awaited
with a feeling of consternation by many
of .the professors and instructors of the
Berkeley seat of learning.
"I gave no orders whatever for the land
ing of the doctor, and never give orders
for the landing of Chinamen," added St.
John. "I simply execute the orders of the
Collector. Customs Inspector Banks was
at the gang plank, and he declares that he
allowed no Chinese persons to pass. Dr.
Yung Wing might easily be mistaken for
a South American, as there was nothing
about him to suggest that he was a Chi
naman and I suppose he walked oft the
steamer without being challenged. The
steamship company is responsible for the
safe keeping of Chinese immigrants on
board and I have nothing to do with
them. Dr. Wing went to the Occidental
Hotel on the same day and was inter
viewed by hotel reporters for the daily
papers and they puolished articles about
him. If the Chinese Bureau wanted him
they could have got him at the hotel. He
left on Sunday morning on his way to Log
Angeles, en route to his home. All the
papers had a. notice of his arrival and I
am astonished that the bureau didn't get
him at the hotel. Just to show you the
way they do business they charged me
with letting the woman Ah Kee escape,
when she was on the steamer all the time
and never left it."
The special agents are investigating the
matter.
The Gaelic arrived last Friday and
among her cabin passengers was Dr.
Wing, a graduate of Yale, a naturalized
American citizen and a resident of New
Haven, Connecticut. On Saturday St.
John was on board the steamer and met
the doctor, whom he found to ' be a ven
erable gentleman of 74 years of age,
dressed in American clothing, wearing a
gray mustache curled up at the ends and
speaking the purest English. The doctor
showed the official his naturalization par
pers and St. John said: "I guess you're
all right on those papers." Then he
turned away to attend to his official du
ties.
There is war between . Deputy Customs
Surveyor' St. John and the Chinese Bu
reau. The bureau charged him with ine
responsibility for the escape of Dr.* Yung
Wing from the Gaelic last Friday and of
Ah Kee. the female nurse in the family
of E. WJ Tilden, agent of the Pacific Mail
Steamship Company at Kobe, Japan. St.
John proved that the woman never left
the vessel and that she is still there, and
the charge was narrowed down to the es
cape of the Chinese doctor. â¢ v

CHICAGO, June 17.â Corn went on the ram
page to-day, and, headed by 'the bull crowd
that has cornered July options,' led the un
fortunate ' shorts a- rapid and exciting chase.
Early in the day the word went around that
the screws were, to .be put on the shorts long
before the settling day. -The pit was in a con
stant' boil from start to finish. It was a
day for. covering and practically everything
else was lost eight of. Thete is little doubt
that the principals back of the corner in July,
which is credited to the Gates and Wall street
crowd, are trying to move out all contract
stuff. The 'country probably could furnish
plenty of stuff with which, to supply the shorts,
but the wet weather has worked against grad
ing. Of to-day's receipts only ten out of 378
cars graded No. 2. Primary receipts were in
creased to 477.000 bushels and private elevators
were making good increases in stock. But all
told there was cause for fear among the shorts,
for, it is thought by old speculators that the
long expected squeeze Is at hand, otherwise
the bulls would not allow the price to run,
away * as it - did to-day. 1 Contract stocks to
day were ' a little over two million bushels.
The bull crowd is credited with .having
15,000,000 bushels of July. Predictions are for
75c corn as a result of . this situation, and
fluctuations to-day warranted such predictions.
July opened unchanged to ] /&c up at 64 v %@O4'5&c,
after having sold yesterday as low -as 63c. A
sharp ten minutes' buying sent the price to
t50%c. The bulls supplied enough long stuff
on profit-taking to let the price slip back to
65%c, but then the shorts became panic
stricken, the market turned erratic and July
shot to 07%c, where it closed very strong,
:j^c higher than yesterday'e close. The Gates
crowd was on both sides of the market, but
sold more than It bought. Deferred futures were
traded in lightly, but influenced somewhat by
the advance in July. Cash- business was not
as good as recently, and country offerings were
small. Cash stuff was selling 5@8c under July
orlces.
Wheat ruled steady. It was strongest early
on higher Paris cables and showers Southwest.
Traders said the wet weather was interfering
with harvesting and for this reason would sÂ«ll
little. The absence of any Â¦ heavy trading
worked against prices and although July corn
strength helped wheat somewhat, fluctuations
were very narow. July opened unchanged to
>/sC up at 72@72>6c and sold to 72? g c.
Light selling caused a dip to 71%@72c an.l
luly closed steady, a shade lower, at 72@72V&c.
Oats were dull and steady, fluctuating within
a range of only ?Â»c. July closed a shade lower,
at 39%c.
Provision trade was almost nil. July pork
closed 7%c down, lard 5c "down and ribs 2%c
lower. Â¦

WHEAT â The markets all over the world

back. Shipping descriptions were unchanged.

OATS â The demoralization of the past three

CORN â There was a sudden flurry at Chi
cago yesterday. A dispatch from Fort Worth.
Texas, said that the drought over Northern and
Central Texas was becoming alarming, the
crops burning up on account of hot winds, and
that unless there were rains in a few day8
the corn crop would be a total failure. This
sudden and unexpected dry weather scare sent
the shorts skurrying for cover, and advanced
the market from 64%c to 6f9gc.
