Butter, Cheese and Eggs.
Butter continues , weak, and sellers are giv
ing concessions to effect sales. Stocks are too
large for the light local demand to absorb.
Cheese remains as previously quoted. Sup
plies are not excessive, but the market baa
about all it can carry. - - ,
Eggs are undeniably weak, and dealers are
offerings them on all sides at concessions from
previous prices. Good ranch Eggs can now
bo bought for ISc, and 20c is the top asked
by anybody. Supplies are very large and ac
cumulating. Store Eggs are especially weak,,
and are' not in favor with buyers at the mo
ment, as the hot weather in the Interior is
beginning to affect their quality. Receipts are
increasing again, as will be seen. -
Receipts were 58,600 pounds, 18S cubes and

pounds of Oregon Cheese.

Deciduous and Citrus Fruits.
Berries were In heavy receipt, and with the
exception of Gooseberries, which were scarce,
all descriptions were quoted lower. Some poor
Longworth Strawberries were sold at $6 per
chest. A few Logans .were sold In a small
way as high as - Â§0 per chest, but the prices
quoted below cover most of the offerings.
Currants came to hand in good condition and
prices had a narrower range. The canners
were offering $2 25, but were unable to pro
cure any. -.
A crate of Watermelons was received from
Indlo, Riverside County. The crate contained
fourteen fair-sized ripe melons, but had not
been sold up to a late hour. Five crates of
Thompson's Seedless Grapes were received from
the same place, but arrived too late to be sold.
Two crates of the same 'Variety from Yuma
sold for $3 50 per crate.
Choice offerings of Cherries In bulk met with
ready sale, but there was a lot of poor stock
received from the river, which sold slowly at
the bottom quotations.
There is little new to report in summer fruits.
The demand for shipment north did much to
relieve the market, but receipts were large
and prices were generally lower. Nineteen
boxes of choice white Astrakan Apples were
received from Sacramento, but had not been
cold up to a late hour.
Citrus fruits are In light supply and offer
ings sell readily.

! WEATHfiR CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure has fallen rapidly during the
past 24 hours over Oregon and Washington.
A marked depression overlies California and
Nevada and will likely move northward.
The temperature continues high in the in
terior of California. Over Eastern Nevada and
Utah there has Â¦ been a fall of from 8 to 1 1
degrees. The temperature has risen 10 de
grees or more over Oregon and Washington.
No rain has fallen In California, but condi
tions are such that unsettled weather may be
expected on the coast Wednesday.

ending midnisht June 18. 1902:

terior; light northerly winds inland; brisk
westerly winds on the coast, jt

I weather inland Wednesday; fresh northwest
I winds.

GENERAL SUMMARY.
Warm weather has prevailed in the central

ifornia the -weather has been unusually cool,

done to crops. Farmers are burning pasture
lands and spraying orchards and vines in an
â¢ffort to destroy the pest. In some places it
la claimed that grain is tco far advanced to be
â¢eriously injured by the grasshoppers. Grain
harvest is progressing nicely in the great val
leys. Barley is yielding very well, and the
au&llty * 6 excellent. Very little wheat has been
harvested, but the crop is maturing rapidly.

FAMILY MERGER
SOLVES PROBLEM
OAKLAND, June 13.â W. F. Rudolph, a.
well-known promoter of 960 Eighteenth
street, visited the Elks' Fair with his
wife last night, having first locked his
house doors. On Rudolph's returning to
his residence at midnight he was sur
prised to see a light in one of the upper
rooms. Concluding that some one had
effected an entrance, he went into nia
kitchen, and arming himself with a.
butcher knife, started to go upstairs. A
man disguised in a mask met him, and.
drawing a revolver, took a shot at Ru
dolph, but missing him, struck him a vio
lent blow with the weapon, knocking him
down the stairs.
The burglar then made good his escape
and Rudolph, as soon as he recovered
from the blow and the fall, gave the
alarm to the police.
A search was at once set on foot, put
up to an early hour this morning no ar-Â»
rest had been made.
BURGLAR VISITS HOUSE
WHII.E OWNER IS AT FAIR
SEATTLE, June 17.â The servant girl
problem has at last been solved by some
of the citizens ot Ballard, a suburban
town, who have formed a co-operative
club, numbering eight families. Officers
have been elected and a commodious
building engaged, which will serve as
kitchen and dining-rooms. Competent
help will be hired, and, although all will
bear the expenses of the undertaking,
each family will have its own table in tho
dining-room and enjoy as much as possi
ble the privacy and comforts of a home.
Although this departure in the merger
business is only an experiment it bids fair
to become a success. One of the rules of
the club will be that no "knocker" will bo
tolerated. All complaints must bo made
to the president. It is expected that tho
scheme will be In running order by an
other week. '
Households Consolidate and
Form a Co- Operative
Olub.
Ballard Polk Remedy
the Servant Girl
Question.

NAPA. June 17.â The Grand Grove of
Druids began its session to-day. Noble
Grand Arch Julius Godeau presiding. All
of the grand officers are in attendance.
The committee on credentials and Past
Supreme Arch L. S. Schord conferred the
past arch degree upon seventy-seven no
vitiates. , .
At the afternoon session the time was
devoted to the reports of the noble grand
arch and the grand officers. They showed
2173 additions to the membership during
the past year. Novato Grove reports. a
300 per cent gain. Eleven new groves have
been instituted during the term.
Several amendments were presented to
be voted upon, changing the. basis of rep
resentation in the Grand Grove. The noble
grand arch recommended that the Cali
fornia Druid be adopted as the official or
gan. ' â¢ . ' Â¦
The case of Olympia Grove No. 119 was
brought up, but the Grand Grove decided
to take it up on Wednesday afternoon,
and meanwhile the Grand Grove will not
let the delegates from that grove take
their seats. .
To-night a reception was tendered the
visiting delegates by the Napa Druids.

Pretty Booths Where Dainty Viands
Are Dispensed to Visitors.
OAKLAND, June 17.â The sudden rise in
the thermometer to-day created a large
and insatiable thirst among the visiting
crowds at the fair and the several refresh
ment booths did a rushing business in
icecream and lemonade.
At the Squirrel Inn. hot biscuits and
coffee were shelved for the time being
and the ladles devoted their energies to
the preparation of cooler refreshments.
Mrs. A. A. Sangster presided as hostess
to-day and was assisted by Mrs. D. T.
Fowler. Miss Jennie Wheaton, Mrs.
Charles Emery and Miss Lucille Haber.
To-morrow Mrs. F. A. Ring will be in
charge
The doughnuts at the Fabiola booth are
still in demand.
The emergency hospital, next door,
which is also conducted under the aus
pices of the Fabiola Association, is one
of the prettiest places on the ground.
Everything is so clean and dainty that it
is almost a temptation to get hurt. Two
trained nurses and a doctor are always
in attendance and the number of cases
that have been treated there have al
ready proved the necessity of a hospital.
IVUDYABD IS WROTH.
AT ROTTINDEAN FOLK
LONDON, June 17.â The action of Rud
yard Kipling on June 2 in dispersing a
mob which threatened the house at Rot
tindean, Sussex, of his aunt, Lady
Burne-Jbnes, a pro-Boer and widow of
the late Sir Edward Burne-Jones, the
painter, because of her having dis
played a black banner inscribed, "You
have killed, you have conquered," has
caused a local sensation 'that shows no
signs of abating.- Â¦ x _ wl
When Kipling, who also lives at Rottln
dean, went to the rescue of his aunt and
in so doing shouldered his way through
the crowd, he delivered a thoroughly Kip
lingesque oration of the kind the people
of Rottlndean were not accustomed to
hear from one who had established the
local rifle brigade and had stirred up local
imperialism to the boiling Doint. To show
hi? further disapproval of the attack on
his aunt's house Kipling has now shut
up the drill hall which he had given to
the publlo. This Â¦ action has created in
tense feeling. Kipling. refuses to express
an opinion regarding the black banner,
but he emphatically announces his dis
approval of Rottindean's manner of ex
pressing its disapproval of it.

SONOMA YOUNG COUPLE
SURPRISE RELATIVES
Granddaughter of General Vallejo a
Principal in a Romantic
Marriage.
SANTA ROSA. June 17.â Anita Vallejo
Emparen, a pretty little maiden of 19
summers, granddaughter of the late Gen
eral Mariana Guadalupe Vallejo, was
wedded in this city to-day to Dr. Allen
Milo Thompson. The couple had been en
gaged only a short time and none else
knew of it but the mother of the bride
to-be, Mrs. Lulu Emparen. The wedding
was. set for a distant date, but with the
impetuosity of young lovers they decided
to hasten the event. Arriving this fore
nobn from Sonoma,- the birthplace and
iift-long home of the bride, they present
ed themselves to Deputy County Cle^k
William Mobley and obtained a license.
Dr. Thompson then sought Justice of the
Peace John Brown and conducted him to
the parlors of the Occidental Hotel, where
the marriage vows were pronounced.
It was a sort of a runaway wedding and
in the afternoon the couple returned for
the parental blessing. They expect to
have a repetition of the ceremony to-mor
row by Father Leahy, the priest In charge
of the pretty little church of St. Francis
at Sonoma.
Mrs. Thompson is one of the handsom
est young women of all Sonoma County
Bht: possesses the beauty and the grace
of her Castilllan ancestors. Dr. Thomp
son is a practicing physician of. Sonoma.

The exhibits of the Elks themselves
show great ingenuity and humor. The
Elks police court, which was started last
night for the first time and will be con
tinued through the week, is full of fun.
Burlesque policemen are sent out through
the ground to arrest any Elks and certain
of the well-known men about town. These
arrested ones are taken before an Elks
police judge and fined for anything or
nothing, generally the latter. Last night
"Jedge" G. Wash. Reed sat, while to
night J. Rastus McElroy presided and
dealt out injustice to all. The Elkville po-
depend almost exclusively upon volunteer
assistance for their officials and were not
prepared to handle such crowds as have
patronized the /carnival. Chief of Police
Hodgkins has 'been on the grounds every
evening, while the actual handling of the
patrolmen has been in the control of
Night Police Captain Walter J. Petersen.
Clay Hawbaker says he is going to clear
enough money from the big Elks and
soda water to buy the "Elks' family" for
the new lodgreroom.
The Elks' Country Store has afforded as
much amusement as any feature on the
grounds. Some of the articles that are se
cured through the blind prize ticket sys
tem of purchase are extremely startling.
The Native Sons will run riot to-morrow
night and on Thursday night the Wood-

The San Francisco Turn Verein is mak
ing great preparations for participating
in the gymnastic and field contests com
ing off at the district tournament to be
held at San Jose, commencing Saturday,
June 21, and ending June 24. There will
be delegations from all the vereins on the
coart from Portland to San Diego, and
the contest for the first honors will be
exciting and interesting. The classes of
the local verein have been in active train
ingr under Professor H. Goetz for more
than two months.
The contests include apparatus work,
field sports, recitations, singing and
sharpshootlng. Two trains have been
chartered by the verein to carry its mem
bers and friends to the scene of the con
tests. One will leave Saturday evening
at 6:30 o'clock and the other on Sunday
ir.ornir.g at 8:30 o'clock. A rousing recep
tion awaits the participants at San Jose.
The affair is in charge of the following
committee: F. Otter, H. Roehr and H.
Hemtning of San Jose; J. Simmen, H.
Goetz, William Plagemann, C. Trost, F.
Zecher and William Bamgarten of San
Francisco: A- Curlin of Oakland, and J.
Simon of Stockton.

TURN VEREIN PREPARES
FOR A BIG TOURNAMENT
The crowds are uniformly good-natured.
The police have had little trouble in con
trolling them and the arrangements have
been very good considering that the Elks
GOOD-NATURED CROWDS.
Not a small number have been drawn
from San Francisco, while the neighbor
ing towns have contributed liberally to
ward the numbers who have Invaded Oak
land. This is regarded with favor by peo
ple who are interested in the material ad
vancement of Oakland, for it indicates an
awakening on the part of thJs city and an
ability to attract from the outside.
OAKLAND, June 17.â This was a
quiet, though none the less suc
cessful day at the Elks' Car
nival. There was no set pro
gramme except the music that
is furnished every afternoon and evening,,
and those who attended had time to wan
der about and visit the concessions, some
thing that the abnormal crowds of Satur
day and Monday made almost impossible.
The success of the carnival is assured and
has gone beyond even the wildest expec
tations of the Oakland Elks. The radical
members of the Elks thought that 50.000
people might visit the fair during the
seven days, while the conservative mem
bers thought that the number might be
as low as 25,000. The paid admissions on
Saturday numbered 16,000, while yesterday
they reached the enormous number of
21,874. Thus in two days the extreme
number of estimated admissigns for the
entire season has been almost reached.
It is now believed that the total number
of paid admissions during the week will
exceed 100,000.
Trials Furnish
Much Fun.
Appoints White Guardian.
Secretary M. J. White of the California
Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to
Children was granted letters of guardian
ship over Alice, Gertrude and William
Murray and Mabel Simmons yesterday.
The mother of the children was sent to
the County Jail for three months recently
for neglecting her little ones. They have
been at the Children's Hospital since her
arrest.
J. P. Mullen, second assistant engineer
of the steamer Pomona, was taken before
United States Court Commissioner Hea
cock yesterday morning for Identification
on a charge of having forged a postal
money t>rd<?r payable to John J. Searey.
Thursday. June 19, was set as the date lor
the hearing and bail was fixed in the sum
of $2000.
Hullen Up for Forg-ery.
Douglas S. Cone of Red Bluff was in
Ean Francisco yesterday. He announced
definitely that he was not a candidate for
nomination to the office of Railroad Com
missioner.
Bank Commissioner Bernard Murphy,
the leading light of the Monticello Club,
appears to be gaining favor in the Demo
cratic camp. In their own councils the
Democrats contend that Murphy could
easily defeat Gage. Dick Beamer is
spoken of as a chief among vote-getting
statesman of the Democratic party. When
the question of winning out against Pres
ton, Schmitz. Pardee, Flint or Edson is
presented, the Democrats make no pre
dictions of success. It is not at all like
ly that the Republican State Convention
will go to the leaders of the Democratic
party for advice in the make-up of the
ticket. The Republican voters desire the
nomination of a man whose leadership
will unite their own party and strengthen
their imn tiekctf-i. â¢"
The story that ex-Governor Budd is
Elated for Democratic leadership in the
gubernatorial campaign cannot be 6up
pressed by the repeated assertions of
Budd that under no circumstances will he
accept the nomination.
THE DEMOCRATIC CAMP.
The various candidates for legislative
honors are wisely beeping out of the
gubernatorial contest Before the San
Jose convention there was an inclination
on the part of push-sympathizing aspir
ants to get next to the Gage machine,
but since the crushing defeat of the boss
forces at a. point where Gage had put
forth his utmost endeavor to win, the
legislative aspirants have been steering
away from his Gageship. . The desire for
Gage's nomination comes mainly from the
Democratic camp. The Governor is now
actively bidding for the support of Demo
crats and points to the fact that he has
retained in office and reaupointed many
representatives of the Democratic party.
In the Twenty-second Senatorial Dis
trict, comprising the Thirty-ninth and
Fortieth Assembly districts, San Fran
cisco, there is promise of a hot fight for
the Republican nomination. The contest
ants in the Held are Hamilton Bauer, Big
Bettman, Daniel Ryan, E. B. Read. Ed
Beeler and Jacob Samuels.
Republican candidates for the Legisla
ture are coming to the front. In Sonoma
County Victor Piezza. of Santa. Rosa is
spoken of for the Assembly to represent
the Fourteenth District. In the Thir
teenth or Petaluma district there is talk
of giving the Republican nomination to F.
A. Cromwell. Sonoma County elects a
State Senator this year to succeed J. C.
Pirns. The Republicans expect to win
Judge R. F. Crawford of Santa Rosa is
mentioned as among the leading aspir
ants.
LEGISLATIVE ASPIRANTS.
The name of Gage was not mentioned in
the Republican Convention, of Tulare
County, held at Visalia. Resolutions were
adopted indorsing Thomas Flint for Gov
ernor and S. C. Smith for Congress.
Two weeks ago the machine politicians
were figuring that Gage would have 150
delegates in the State convention and
with such transferable strength would bo
able to dictate nominations. Reports
from the interior indicate that 150 is an
overestimate of the machine following.
One hundred will be nearer the mark and
Gage will fall below that number if he
fails to win out in Los Angeles. The
original purpose of the push to make an
open fight tor Gage delegates in San
Francisco has been abandoned. No doubt
efforts will be made to secretly run in a
lew delegates favorable to his renomina
tion. but the voters will be on their guard
to checkmate this game.

COMMERCIAL NEWS
Kittle Smith, alias Myrtle Reece,
stewardess on the steamer Mohican, who
was convicted by Judge Mogan Monday
of disturbing the peace, "was sentenced
yestr*day~to serve three months* In the
County Jail, which is the limit for the
offense. Frank Noonan, the substitute
fireman who neglected hi3 wife and two
children for the stewardess and threaten
ed his wife if she testified against the
prisoner, was brought before the Judga
and received a severe reprimand. Mrs.
Noonan- asked the Judge to give him an
other chance to behave himself and the
Judge consented. . .

FUNERAL REFORM BOYCOTT. '
The undertakers of Pah Francisco are un-
friendly to cremation because the expense 13
very much less than that of interment. They
know that if cremation sets thr? pace in fu-
neral reform reduced prices must be met, or
graveyards closed. They over five
hundred hacks, the drivers of which belong
to the Hackmen's Union. Local No. .224. That
union Uhrouoh misrepresentation j made . to
them) has resolved not to drive for us at our
funerals or rent hacks to attend them.
We think the friends of cremation wilt, if
necessary, dispense with, a string of, hack*
and the services of the undertakers rather
than see incineration obstructed by a cause-
less, heartless boycott of the dead.
We own two first-class carriages and at-
tractive hearses and horses, which we will
furnish upon application. For further . in-
formation-, call at .No.. 42ft Golden Gate ave.

The trial of Albert Hornlein of the Cafe
Royal, charged with battery upon M.
Fass, was held before Judge Fritz yester
day. Fass declared that when he asked
Hornlein for a dollar Hornlein took hold
of him and kicked him out of the place.'
The defendant and a number of witnesses
testified that Fass was only taken by the
arm and led out of the place. The case
was continued till to-day and Judge jptHz
said that If the defendant could show by
the rules of the club that he had a right
to put Fass out the case would be dis
missed. If he did not have that right he
would only be guilty of technical battery

A. M. Cameron/former lieutenant in the
Fifth Cavalry and later connected with
the Police Department in Manila, ap
peared before Judge Fritz yesterday on a
charge of assault to murder for firing two
shots Saturday j nignt at Fred Hawes,
night clerk in the Winchester House.
Hawes was at nrst disinclined to swear to
a complaint, but finally 'consented.
It was not denied that Cameron fired the
shots, but it was urged for the defense
that ho was liquor-crazed. The shots
were fired into the elevator shaft and it
was claimed that they were fired simply
with the intention of frightening Hawes.
Cameron is dying from tuberculosis and
was on his way from Manila to a sani
tarium in New Mexico with the hope of
prolonging his life when arrested. He
showed numerous letters testifying
to his good character while serving in the
army, in the revenue service and in the
Manila Pollc# Department. His father
was a colonel in thelarmy. -'
Judge Fritz, in view of Cameron's pre
vious good character and his physical
condition, Allowed him to go on his own
recognizance and continued the case for
six months.

Charles Soxton.a messenger boy with the
American District Telephone Company, was
sentenced to thirty days' imprisonment in
the County Jail by Judge Fritz yesterday
on a charge of petty larceny. He an
swered a call Monday to a house on
Taylor street and received a dispatch to
bo sent East and a dollar for charges. An
answer to the dispatch did not come as
expected and inquiry -elicited the fact
that Soxton had torn up the message and
kept the dollar. J. M. Corcoran, the
manager of the company, said that boys
were in the habit of doing that sort of
thing and he wanted an example made of
Soxton. , .. , -

CF. RICHARDS, a pioneer. drug
merchant of this city, died last
Sunday afternoon at his resi-
O dence, SOI ' Pennsylvania ave
nue. Death was due to apo
plexy, and was entirely unexpected.,Rich
ards had but recently returned from a.
trip to Yosemite Valley, and up to the
time of his death was apparently in the
best of health. Besides being well known
in the business world, he was also known
in literary circles, as in years past he had
frequently contributed short stories and
poems to the Golden Era and^Alta Cali
fornia, pioneer publications of nhis State.
Richards was born in Redbank, N. J.,
in 1842, and came to California in 1S62. He
was probably best known to the people
of this city in connection with the drug
business, which he conducted for thirty
years at the corner of Clay and Sansome
streets. Recently he removed to 406 Clay
street.
He leaves a wife and four children. The
funeral will be held to-day.
The officials of the Western National
Bank have assured the police that they
will aid in every way if it is determined
to prosecute Block for what he did In this
city. It is possible, however, that Block
may be surrendered to Eastern authori
ties, where his operations assumed great
er magnitude than they did in San Fran
â¢cisco.
The police believe that there will be no
difficulty in proving that Stone and Block
are the same person, although the prison
er claims that the drafts were given him
by another man and that he believed them
to be genuine.
In some respects the prisoner is rather
remarkable. He is well dressed and has
handsome jewelry, and when searched at
the City Prison had $1700 in currency on
his person. He maintains an absolute
silence, regarding himself and hi3 previous
movements, and no amount of questioning
can draw from him any admissions. â¢ Tlje
detectives have not been able to locate
his stopping place in this city, but when
they do they expect to get conclusive evi
dence against him.
A telegram from the National Hotel
keepers' Association at New York, re
ceived yesterday by Captain Martin, ,ask
ed that John O. Stone be held for numer
ous fraudulent transactions, and stating
that Cashier Clark of the Palace would
know the particulars. Clark was unable
to throw any light on the case when ques
tioned last night.
Investigation of the records taken in
connection with papers found on the pris
oner convince the police that Block is
one of a gang which has been victimizing
hotel keepers and banks through the
Southern and Middle Western States for a
year past. They are said to have cleared
up $75 000 by their operations.
On the prisoner's person were found a
number of drafts for large sums on banks
in Indianapolis, Cleveland, New York and
other cities, payable to R. M. Jones, G. A.
Graves, John O. Stone and other names.
These names in! many instances corre
spond with those on bogus drafts drawn
in the name of the Earl Fruit and Pro
duce Company of California, on the Amer
ican Bank and Trust Company of San
Francisco and the First National Bank of
San Jose. These drafts have been scat
tered broadcast throughout the United
States, and fully $100,000 has been realized.
The swindle has been going on foe many
months past, and the police of nearly all
the large cities in the United States have
been endeavoring unsuccessfully to locate
the criminals. On the back of one of the
drafts found on the prisoner was written
the name of the Earl Fruit and Produce
Company. . â¢â¢ ;
According to the police, Block came to
San Francisco about two weeks ago and
at once commenced laying his plans lor
a, large banK swindle, jtie selected tne
western iNational Bank as a victim and
a tew days ago opened an account there
by depositing $17i>. .Later he deposited a
draft for $1000 pn the Springrield National
Bank of bpringfleld, Mass., payable to J.
O. fatone, and two cnecKs aggregating |9uO
on the Bank of Santa Clara at San Jose.
Against this account he drew a check
last Saturday for $35, which was paid, -and
on Monday he presented a check for $20U0.
which, nearly exhausted his credit, but
which the bank officials refused to cash.
In the meantime they had telegraphed to
the Sprinttgeld, Mass., bank and ascer
tained that the $1000 draft was forged, and
Monday afternoon the two checKs came
back from San Jose protested. The police
were, immediately notified and the detec
tives promptly located Block and took
him into custody.
Shortly- after he was arrested a telegram
was received from the city marshal of
Springfield. Mass., asking for the arrest
of J. O. Stone, and giving a description
that exactly suited Block. From this it
is supposed that the Springfield bank has
already been victimized by him, and that
he is known there. â¢
As was published exclusively in The
Call,- the arrest of J. Block by Detectives
George McMahon and Ross Whittaker
Monday night has developed into an im
portant capture. Block was taken in cus
tody at the request of Cashier Scott of the
Western National Bank of this city, and
his identity 'and , the reason for his de
tention were carefully suppressed by the
police until last night, when Captain- of
Detectives Martin gave publicity to the
facts in the case.
The point of respondents is correct and la
supported by authority. Under out theory of
government, there is no property right in an
of flee; offices are considered to subserve the
public interests and are not for the benefit
of the occupant thereof, and when the term
is not fixed by law, there is no reason why the
power to appoint should not also possess the
power to remove at its pleasure and such as
shown, has been the practice.
Dr. Sponogle was appointed superin
tendent of Agnews Insane Asylum on
April 25. 1S97. On August 23, 1833, he was
dismissed without a hearing and Dr. J.
H. Crane put in his place. Sponogle made
a petition to the Superior Court of Santa
Clara county for a writ of certiorari
compelling the managers of the asylum
to reinstate him. The petition was denied
and an appeal was taken to the Supreme
Court. The contention of the appellant
was that he could not legally have been
removed except for cause and after
charges had been preferred and he had
been given an- opportunity to make re
ply and, secondly, that his term of office
having once been appointed, was for four
years. The managers claimed on the
other hand that the law which denies the
right of removal except for cause was
unconstitutional and, in regard to the
length of the term of office, that the
lunacy law of 1897 superseded all pre
vious enactments on the subject. The
Supreme Court sustains the position of
the managers as follows:
I concur in the Judgment. The Injunction
order In Crowley vs. Freud prohibits the Civil
Service Commission from holding examinations
or the employes of the Recorder â such as copy
ists and stenographers â as well as his deputies.
The Recorder is therefore driven to the neces
sity of employing such assistants without civil
service examinations or not at all: and unless
such employes can be paid, the business of the
office would be practically suspended. I do not,
however, think that the employes referred to
are deputies within the meaning of the law;
nor is it necessary to so hold in order to
affirm the Judgment. â¢
The dispute as to the status of copy
ists was raised by Treasurer Brooks in
refusing to pay W. H. Garnett for one
month's labors in the Recorder's office.
The position of several other employes in
the same office depended on the settle
ment of the dispute. The Supreme Court
affirms without reservation the Judgment
of the lower court and quotes a large
part of the former decision as the ground
work for its own opinion.
In the case of Crowley vs. Freud, the
Supreme Court declared that the deputies
of the Recorder could not be appointed
by the Civil Service Commission. In the
piesent case the contention of the com
mission was that copyists are not depu
ties. It is held by the court that depu
ties need not "be considered in any nar
row sense, but are in reality any and all
subordinates in any county office. The
opinion is written by Justice McFarland,
with whom Chief Justice Beatty and Jus
tices Garoutte and Temple directly con
cur. Jjustice Van Dyke writes the follow
ing concurring opinion:
The Supreme Court decided yesterday
that the Recorder and not the Civil Ser
vice Commission has the right to apooint
copyists in the office of the former and
that the Treasurer must cash the salary
â¢warrants of such employes. The Board
of Manager a of the Agnews State Hos
pital for the Insane were also upheld in
ousting Dr. F. M. Sponogle from the su
perintendency of that .institution in 18tt9.
Papers Pound on Him Indi
cate That He Has a
Remarkable Record.
